    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Primula vulgaris (primrose) genome assembly, annotation and gene expression, with comparative genomics on the heterostyly supergene"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2018 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-12-18"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Primula vulgaris (primrose) exhibits heterostyly: plants produce self-incompatible pin- or thrum-form flowers, with anthers and stigma at reciprocal heights. Darwin concluded that this arrangement promotes insect-mediated cross-pollination; later studies revealed control by a cluster of genes, or supergene, known as the S (Style length) locus. The P. vulgaris S locus is absent from pin plants and hemizygous in thrum plants (thrum-specific); mutation of S locus genes produces self-fertile homostyle flowers with anthers and stigma at equal heights. Here, we present a 411&#8201;Mb&#8201;P. vulgaris genome assembly of a homozygous inbred long homostyle, representing ~87% of the genome. We annotate over 24,000&#8201;P. vulgaris genes, and reveal more genes up-regulated in thrum than pin flowers. We show reduced genomic read coverage across the S locus in other Primula species, including P. veris, where we define the conserved structure and expression of the S locus genes in thrum. Further analysis reveals the S locus has elevated repeat content (64%) compared to the wider genome (37%). Our studies suggest conservation of S locus genetic architecture in Primula, and provide a platform for identification and evolutionary analysis of the S locus and downstream targets that regulate heterostyly in diverse heterostylous species."/>

Darwin concluded that this arrangement promotes insect-mediated cross-pollination; later studies revealed control by a cluster of genes, or supergene, known as the S (Style length) locus.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Primula vulgaris</i> (primrose) exhibits heterostyly: plants produce self-incompatible pin- or thrum-form flowers, with anthers and stigma at reciprocal heights. Darwin concluded that this arrangement promotes insect-mediated cross-pollination; later studies revealed control by a cluster of genes, or supergene, known as the <i>S</i> (<i>Style length</i>) locus. The <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus is absent from pin plants and hemizygous in thrum plants (thrum-specific); mutation of <i>S</i> locus genes produces self-fertile homostyle flowers with anthers and stigma at equal heights. Here, we present a 411 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly of a homozygous inbred long homostyle, representing ~87% of the genome. We annotate over 24,000 <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genes, and reveal more genes up-regulated in thrum than pin flowers. We show reduced genomic read coverage across the <i>S</i> locus in other <i>Primula</i> species, including <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i>, where we define the conserved structure and expression of the <i>S</i> locus genes in thrum. Further analysis reveals the <i>S</i> locus has elevated repeat content (64%) compared to the wider genome (37%). Our studies suggest conservation of <i>S</i> locus genetic architecture in <i>Primula</i>, and provide a platform for identification and evolutionary analysis of the <i>S</i> locus and downstream targets that regulate heterostyly in diverse heterostylous species.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Floral heteromorphy in <i>Primula</i> has been studied for over 150 years. Charles Darwin first recognized the importance of this breeding system for promoting cross-pollination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Darwin, C. R. On the two forms or dimorphic condition in the species of Primula, and on their remarkable sexual relations. Journal of the Proceedings of the Linnean Society, Botany 6, 77–96 (1862)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d1397e503">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Darwin, C. R. The Different Forms of Flowers on Plants of the Same Species (John Murray, 1877)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1397e506">2</a></sup>; observations on its existence date back even further<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gilmartin, P. M. On the origins of observations on heterostyly in Primula. New Phytologist 208, 39–51 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d1397e510">3</a></sup>. In heterostylous <i>Primula</i> species, plants produce one of two forms of flower, pin or thrum, with anthers and stigma in reciprocal positions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1</a>). This arrangement physically promotes insect-mediated outcrossing between the two floral morphs. Pin flowers present the stigma at the mouth of the corolla tube, and the anthers halfway down (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1a,f,d,i</a>). Thrum flowers present anthers at the mouth of the flower, and the stigma halfway down (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1b,g,e,j</a>). In most <i>Primula</i> species, this physical mechanism functions alongside a pollen-pistil recognition self-incompatibility (SI) system, which inhibits self-fertilization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lewis, D. Comparative incompatibility in angiosperms and fungi. Advances in Genetics Incorporating Molecular Genetic Medicine 6, 235–285 (1954)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d1397e530">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lewis, D. &amp; Jones, D. A. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 129–150 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1397e530_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dowrick, V. P. J. Heterostyly and homostyly in Primula obconica. Heredity 10, 219–236 (1956)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1397e533">6</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="756" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><i>Primula</i> floral phenotypes and angiosperm phylogeny. Floral phenotypes showing face and longitudinal flower sections of <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin (<b>a</b>,<b>f</b>), thrum (<b>b</b>,<b>g</b>) and long homostyle (<b>c</b>,<b>h</b>); <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> pin (<b>d</b>,<b>i</b>) and thrum (<b>e</b>,<b>j</b>); scale bar, 1 cm. Schematic phylogeny of angiosperm species (<b>k</b>) adapted from <a href="https://genomevolution.org/wiki/index.php/Sequenced_plant_genomes#Phylogenetic_Tree">https://genomevolution.org/wiki/index.php/Sequenced_plant_genomes#Phylogenetic_Tree</a> and <a href="http://tolweb.org">http://tolweb.org</a>. Heterostylous species are underlined, species with sequenced and assembled genomes shown in bold.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Darwin investigated the effects of cross and self-fertilization in numerous species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Darwin, C. R. The Effects of Cross and Self Fertilisation in the Vegetable Kingdom (John Murray, 1876)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d1397e621">7</a></sup>; he observed reduced seed set from within-morph crosses of <i>Primula</i>, and proceeded to define heterostyly as a mechanism to ensure outcrossing, and avoid the potential ill-effects of inbreeding on height, vigour and fertility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Darwin, C. R. The Different Forms of Flowers on Plants of the Same Species (John Murray, 1877)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d1397e628">2</a></sup>. This remarkable floral innovation is a striking example of convergent evolution, having evolved independently on at least 23 occasions, in 28 angiosperm families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Lloyd, D. G. &amp; Webb, C. J. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 151–175 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d1397e632">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Barrett, S. &amp; Shore, J. In Self-Incompatibility in Flowering Plants: Evolution, Diversity and Mechanisms (ed V. E. Franklin-Tong) 3–32 (Springer, 2008)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d1397e635">9</a></sup>. Heterostylous <i>Primula</i> species dominate the Primulaceae family, which resides in the order Ericales of the asterids lineage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1k</a>). Development of features specific to each floral morph (heterostyly) is controlled by the <i>S (Style length)</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lewis, D. &amp; Jones, D. A. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 129–150 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1397e649">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bateson, W. &amp; Gregory, R. P. On the inheritance of heterostylism in Primul&#xA;                           a. Proceedings of the Royal Society B 76, 581–586 (1905)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1397e652">10</a></sup>, which is distinct from the self-incompatibility (<i>S</i>) locus described in a number of homomorphic self-incompatible species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Hiscock, S. J. &amp; Tabah, D. A. The different mechanisms of sporophytic self-incompatibility. Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society B 358, 1037-1045 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d1397e659">11</a></sup>. <i>Primula vulgaris</i> and the closely-related <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> have 11 chromosome pairs (2n = 22). <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> has a predicted genome size by flow-cytometry of 459 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Temsch, E. M., Temsch, W., Ehrendorfer-Schratt, L. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Heavy metal pollution, selection, and genome size: the species of the Žerjav study revisited with flow cytometry. Journal of Botany 2010, 596542 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1397e679">12</a></sup> or 489 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Siljak-Yakovlev, S. et al. Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Advanced Science Letters 3, 190–213 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1397e683">13</a></sup>, giving a mean of 474 Mb, which is comparable to 479 Mb predicted for <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Siljak-Yakovlev, S. et al. Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Advanced Science Letters 3, 190–213 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1397e693">13</a></sup>; a <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> genome assembly covering ~65% of the 479 Mb genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e703">14</a></sup> allowed differential expression analysis of a limited set of genes and cross-species comparisons, but did not facilitate assembly of the complete <i>S</i> locus.</p><p>Other commonly studied distylous genera include <i>Fagopyrum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yasui, Y. et al. S-LOCUS EARLY FLOWERING 3 is exclusively present in the genomes of short-styled buckwheat plants that exhibit heteromorphic self-incompatibility. Plos One 7, e31264 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1397e716">15</a></sup>, <i>Turnera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Labonne, J. D. J. &amp; Shore, J. S. Positional cloning of the s haplotype determining the floral and incompatibility phenotype of the long-styled morph of distylous Turnera subulata. Molecular Genetics and Genomics 285, 101–111 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1397e722">16</a></sup> and <i>Linum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ushijima, K. et al. Isolation of the floral morph-related genes in heterostylous flax (Linum grandiflorum): the genetic polymorphism and the transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulations of the S locus. Plant Journal 69, 317–331 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1397e728">17</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1k</a>). The role of heterostyly is an important consideration in attempts to produce true-breeding (homozygous) cultivars of <i>Fagopyrum esculentum</i> (buckwheat) for example: an assembly of the <i>F</i>. <i>esculentum</i> genome was recently reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yasui, Y. et al. Assembly of the draft genome of buckwheat and its applications in identifying agronomically useful genes. DNA Research 23, 535–546 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d1397e745">18</a></sup>, with a view to identifying genes of agronomic significance in this food crop, such as those at the <i>S</i> locus. <i>Primula</i> are important horticultural (ornamental) crops in Europe, the United States and Japan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Karlsson, M. Primula culture and production. HortTechnology 11, 627–635 (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d1397e756">19</a></sup>. For <i>Primula sieboldii</i>, genetic maps have been constructed to facilitate both conservation studies and the identification of genes underpinning the impressive floral variety of this species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Yoshida, Y. et al. QTL analysis of heterostyly in Primula sieboldii and its application for morph identification in wild populations. Annals of Botany 108, 133–142 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d1397e763">20</a></sup>. The availability of a high coverage <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome sequence will facilitate generation of an increased number of markers associated with favourable traits in horticultural cultivars, and also accelerate investigations into the developmental control and convergent evolution of heterostyly.</p><p>Despite the historical role of <i>Primula</i> species in establishing modern genetic theory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Bateson, W. &amp; Gregory, R. P. On the inheritance of heterostylism in Primul&#xA;                           a. Proceedings of the Royal Society B 76, 581–586 (1905)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d1397e779">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bridges, C. B. The chromosome hypothesis of linkage applied to cases in sweetpeas and Primula. American Naturalist 48, 524–534 (1914)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d1397e782">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bodmer, W. F. The genetics of homostyly in populations of Primula vulgaris. Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society B 242, 517–549 (1960)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d1397e782_1">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fisher, R. A. A theoretical system of selection for homostyle Primula. Sankhya 9, 325–342 (1949)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d1397e782_2">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Haldane, J. B. S. Heterostylism in natural populations of the Primrose. Primula acaulis. Biometrika 30, 196–198 (1938)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d1397e785">24</a></sup>, and a range of heterostyly studies in different angiosperm families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yasui, Y. et al. S-LOCUS EARLY FLOWERING 3 is exclusively present in the genomes of short-styled buckwheat plants that exhibit heteromorphic self-incompatibility. Plos One 7, e31264 (2012)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d1397e789">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Labonne, J. D. J. &amp; Shore, J. S. Positional cloning of the s haplotype determining the floral and incompatibility phenotype of the long-styled morph of distylous Turnera subulata. Molecular Genetics and Genomics 285, 101–111 (2011)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d1397e789_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ushijima, K. et al. Isolation of the floral morph-related genes in heterostylous flax (Linum grandiflorum): the genetic polymorphism and the transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulations of the S locus. Plant Journal 69, 317–331 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d1397e792">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Cocker, J. et al. Oakleaf: an S locus-linked mutation of Primula vulgaris that affects leaf and flower development. New Phytologist 208, 149–161 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d1397e795">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, J. et al. Integration of genetic and physical maps of the Primula vulgaris S locus and localization by chromosome in situ hybridisation. New Phytologist 208, 137–148 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1397e798">26</a></sup>, the molecular basis of the phenomenon has remained elusive. However, we recently reported the complete sequence of the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus as a thrum-specific genomic region comprising a cluster of five genes (expressed only in thrum)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e809">27</a></sup>; it is absent from pin, and hemizygous in thrums, not heterozygous as previously assumed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lewis, D. &amp; Jones, D. A. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 129–150 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1397e813">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dowrick, V. P. J. Heterostyly and homostyly in Primula obconica. Heredity 10, 219–236 (1956)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1397e816">6</a></sup>. Occasionally, homostyle <i>Primula</i> plants arise, with anthers and stigma at the same height<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lewis, D. &amp; Jones, D. A. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 129–150 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1397e823">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dowrick, V. P. J. Heterostyly and homostyly in Primula obconica. Heredity 10, 219–236 (1956)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1397e826">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e829">27</a></sup>. These were thought to arise through recombination between dominant and recessive <i>S</i> locus alleles in thrum plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Lewis, D. &amp; Jones, D. A. In Evolution and Function of Heterostyly (ed. Barrett, S. C. H.) 129–150 (Springer Verlag, 1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d1397e836">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Dowrick, V. P. J. Heterostyly and homostyly in Primula obconica. Heredity 10, 219–236 (1956)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d1397e839">6</a></sup>. However, the hemizgous architecture of the <i>S</i> locus precludes such recombination, which suggests that self-fertile homostyle plants must arise through mutation of <i>S</i> locus genes. <i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> and <i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> were identified as the genes controlling anther elevation and style length, respectively, based on such mutations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e862">27</a></sup>. Others have described the function of <i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> (CYP734A50)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Huu, C. N. et al. Presence versus absence of CYP734A50 underlies the style-length dimorphism in primroses. eLife 5, e17956 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1397e873">28</a></sup>, and confirmed our finding that <i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> lies adjacent to <i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Burrows, B. A. &amp; McCubbin, A. G. Sequencing the genomic regions flanking S-linked PvGLO sequences confirms the presence of two GLO loci, one of which lies adjacent to the style-length determinant gene CYP734A50. Plant Reproduction 30, 53–67 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d1397e890">29</a></sup>. The <i>S</i> locus gene <i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> is an apparent duplication of <i>PvGLO</i>, the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> orthologue of the B-function MADS-box gene <i>GLOBOSA</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Li, J. et al. Hose in Hose, an S locus-linked mutant of Primula vulgaris is caused by an unstable mutation at the Globosa locus. PNAS 107, 5664–5668 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d1397e915">30</a></sup>. Our phylogenetic studies revealed an estimated divergence of 51.7 MYA for <i>GLO</i>-<i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e927">27</a></sup>, which suggests a single origin for heterostyly in the Primulaceae, and led us to predict that the <i>S</i> locus structure might be shared amongst <i>Primula</i> species.</p><p>Here, we present an annotated <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly representing ~87% of the predicted 474 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Temsch, E. M., Temsch, W., Ehrendorfer-Schratt, L. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Heavy metal pollution, selection, and genome size: the species of the Žerjav study revisited with flow cytometry. Journal of Botany 2010, 596542 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1397e954">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Siljak-Yakovlev, S. et al. Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Advanced Science Letters 3, 190–213 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1397e957">13</a></sup>. Our assembly is based on a <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> long homostyle plant with high genome-wide homozygosity. We previously reported the 278 kb <i>S</i> locus in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> as a hemizygous complex locus: this region was generated using contigs from our long homostyle, thrum and BAC assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, J. et al. Integration of genetic and physical maps of the Primula vulgaris S locus and localization by chromosome in situ hybridisation. New Phytologist 208, 137–148 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d1397e977">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e980">27</a></sup>. These previous findings raised new questions that we explore in the current manuscript: we address whether the hemizygosity of the <i>S</i> locus and its constituent genes is conserved in different <i>Primula</i> species; given this, we examine genomic features in the non-recombining <i>S</i> locus to determine how unique the region is compared to the wider genome; and, we explore genome-wide differential gene expression, using the comprehensive geneset defined in our <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly, to identify potential direct and indirect downstream targets of this regulatory locus. Our <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly provides a resource for future anchoring to chromosomes, analysis of genome-wide gene families and downstream targets, and investigations on the evolution and function of heterostyly across the Primulaceae and other angiosperm families.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">
                           <i>Primula vulgaris</i> genome assembly</h3><p>To generate the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly (LH_v2), we selected a long homostyle plant originating from the Somerset Wyke Champflower population<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Crosby, J. L. High proportions of homostyle plants in populations of Primula vulgaris. Nature 145, 672–673 (1940)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1029">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Crosby, J. L. Selection of an unfavourable gene complex. Evolutionary Ecology Research 3, 212–230 (1949)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1032">32</a></sup>. <i>P. vulgaris</i> homostyle plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig1">1c,h</a>) are self-fertile<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. Maintenance and breakdown of distyly. American Naturalist 114, 499–513 (1979)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1043">33</a></sup>, producing offspring with greater allelic homozygosity than out-crossed pin or thrum plants. In addition to the opportunity for self-pollination in the natural population, this line underwent further rounds of selfing in cultivation.</p><p>We analysed the <i>k</i>-mer frequency-abundance distribution of genomic paired-end reads (Illumina HiSeq) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>) from the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> long homostyle (Somerset) plant (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig2">2</a>). Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S3</a> contain read libraries corresponding to (unless indicated) Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1a</a> in our previous <i>S</i> locus studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1078">27</a></sup>, and are included here for convenience and completeness. The long homostyle (Somerset) data reveal a unimodal distribution beyond the first local minima, characteristic of a homozygous genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Mapleson, D., Garcia Accinelli, G., Kettleborough, G., Wright, J. &amp; Clavijo, B. J. KAT: a K-mer analysis toolkit to quality control NGS datasets and genome assemblies. Bioinformatics 33, 574–576 (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1082">34</a></sup>. <i>K</i>-mer frequency-abundance distributions for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin and thrum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig2">2</a>) show notable secondary peaks, indicating heterozygosity. We also obtained a second long homostyle from the Chiltern population using maps drawn by Crosby<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Crosby, J. L. Population Genetics in the Genus Primula. (PhD thesis, University of Cambridge (1948)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1099">35</a></sup>. However, <i>k</i>-mer analysis reveals the genome of this individual is more heterozygous than the Somerset homostyle (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig2">2</a>), suggesting more recent outcrossing. The greater homozygosity of the Somerset long homostyle over heterozygous pin and thrum plants, and the Chiltern long homostyle, underpins our <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="571" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genomic sequencing reads <i>k</i>-mer plot. <i>K</i>-mer frequency-abundance distribution (<i>k</i> = 31) generated from <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> paired-end sequencing reads: long homostyle (Somerset) (orange), long homostyle (Chilterns) (blue), thrum (red), and pin (black).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We assembled genomic short read and mate pair sequences from the Somerset <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> long homostyle (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>) using SOAPdenovo v2.04<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 1–6 (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1167">36</a></sup>. Removal of 3,516 non-<i>Primula</i> contigs resulted in 577,740 scaffolds representing 481.3 Mb of sequence with a scaffold-N50 of 236.3 kb; after removal of scaffolds &lt;200 bp in length, the assembly comprised 411.2 Mb of sequence in 67,619 scaffolds. Further processing identified 128 contigs containing chloroplast sequences from <i>Primula</i> species (GenBank accessions KU321892.1, KF753634.1, and KX639823.1), resulting in a final assembly (LH_v2) of 67,491 contigs covering 411.1 (411) Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Tab1">1</a>). We also generated a draft 441.5 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum assembly (VT_v1) using paired-end reads only (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>) (n = 145,617; N50 10.8 kb; NG50 9.5 kb; contigs ≥200 bp). The 411 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> LH_v2 assembly (N50 294.8 kb, NG50 229.8 kb) represents 87% of the estimated 474 Mb genome based on the mean of two flow cytometry estimates for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome size, 459 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Temsch, E. M., Temsch, W., Ehrendorfer-Schratt, L. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Heavy metal pollution, selection, and genome size: the species of the Žerjav study revisited with flow cytometry. Journal of Botany 2010, 596542 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1203">12</a></sup> and 489 Mb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Siljak-Yakovlev, S. et al. Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Advanced Science Letters 3, 190–213 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1207">13</a></sup>; the NG50 was calculated (conservatively) using the higher of these two estimates. NG50 is a more robust metric than N50 that considers the estimated genome size rather than the size of the generated assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Earl, D. et al. Assemblathon 1: a competitive assessment of de novo short read assembly methods. Genome Research 21, 2224–2241 (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1211">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bradnam, K. R. et al. Assemblathon 2: evaluating de novo methods of genome assembly in three vertebrate species. GigaScience 2, 1–31 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1214">38</a></sup>. LH_v2 represents a substantial improvement in contiguity and completeness over a previously published 309.7 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1225">14</a></sup>, which covers 65% of the estimated 479 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Siljak-Yakovlev, S. et al. Towards a genome size and chromosome number database of Balkan flora: C-values in 343 taxa with novel values for 242. Advanced Science Letters 3, 190–213 (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1235">13</a></sup> (N50 165.8 kb, NG50 73.3 kb) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S2</a>). The published <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1249">14</a></sup> contains 40.7 Mb (13.14%) “N”s (ambiguous bases) compared to 29.9 Mb (7.26%) in the 411 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> assembly.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly statistics for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> LH_v2 and gene annotations at various stages of the assembly process.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For assembly validation, we generated <i>k</i>-mer copy number plots (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>). These figures show that the vast majority of <i>k</i>-mers in the genomic sequencing reads are present in the assembly whilst low-frequency <i>k</i>-mers expected to represent sequencing errors, are not. There is minimal change to the observed <i>k</i>-mer spectra when removing contaminated contigs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1b,c</a>) or contigs &lt;200 bp (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1c,d</a>), suggesting these steps did not result in significant loss of genomic content. RNA-Seq reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S2</a>) aligned to the assembly for gene prediction (see below) produced mean overall and concordant pair alignment rates of 91.1% and 85.0%. Furthermore, the assembly includes 97.2% of 248 Core Eukaryotic Genes (CEGs) expected to be present in the majority of eukaryote genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K., Ning, Z., Keane, T. &amp; Korf, I. Assessing the gene space in draft genomes. Nucleic Acids Research 37, 289–297 (2009)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1649">39</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S4</a>). These analyses suggest that most of the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genespace has been captured in the assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repeat sequences in the <i>Primula</i> genome and <i>S</i> locus</h3><p>To analyse repeat sequence composition of the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome we generated a <i>de novo</i> repeat library which revealed 37% of the assembled 411 Mb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome as repetitive; classification of repeat sequences in the LH_v2 genome is detailed in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S5</a>. TEs (transposable elements) comprise over 35% of the genome, which is comparable to the predicted TE content (&gt;35%) in the assembly of the ~389 Mb <i>Oryza sativa</i> (rice) genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1700">40</a></sup>, as well as the 758 Mb assembly of the more closely-related <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (kiwifruit) (36%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nature Communications 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1707">41</a></sup>.</p><p>We used our <i>de novo</i> repeat library to annotate the contiguous 278 kb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1723">27</a></sup>. This revealed the region as particularly rich in TEs (64%), in the top 5% compared to both genome-wide contigs (&gt;10 kb) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S4a</a>), and similarly-sized contigs (278 kb ± 20%) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S4b</a>). In contrast, analysis of 171 kb of sequence flanking the <i>S</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1737">27</a></sup> does not reveal an elevated repeat content (34.67%) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S4a</a>). For the published <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Huu, C. N. et al. Presence versus absence of CYP734A50 underlies the style-length dimorphism in primroses. eLife 5, e17956 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1751">28</a></sup>, the Repbase library<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenetic and Genome Research 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1755">42</a></sup> was used to annotate 7.7% of the published assembly as repetitive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1759">14</a></sup>. Our comprehensive <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> repeat library enabled us to annotate 25% of the published <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1776">14</a></sup>, and 35% of our draft <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> (VT_v1) thrum assembly (contigs &lt;200 bp removed), as repetitive.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">
                           <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> gene annotation</h3><p>Our <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> LH_v2 genome assembly was annotated using RNA-Seq datasets from five tissues (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S2</a>) to predict a total of 24,599 genes; these comprise 29,087 coding sequences, with 4,488 recognised as alternative splice variants. Functional descriptions were assigned to ~85% of genes based on homology to SwissProt, TrEMBL (<a href="http://www.uniprot.org/">http://www.uniprot.org/</a>) and TAIR10 (<a href="https://www.arabidopsis.org/">https://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>) protein databases. Of these, ~90% contain at least one domain, and ~60% are annotated with Gene Ontology (GO) terms. OrthoMCL analysis identified 19,861 orthologous gene groups for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> compared to five angiosperm species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S3</a>).</p><p>To investigate the accuracy of the predicted <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> gene count, we compared coding sequences in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> (24,599) and <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> (18,301)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1856">14</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S3c</a>). We reveal 1,166 <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> coding sequences absent from the <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> assembly, and 6,501 <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> coding sequences absent from <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> gene annotations; the reciprocal analysis reveals 685 <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> coding sequences absent from the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> geneset, and 130 coding sequences absent from <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> contigs. RNA-Seq reads used for <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> gene prediction, produced mean overall and concordant pair alignment rates of 82.5% and 75.7% respectively when mapped to the <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> genome assembly, compared to 91.1% and 85.0% for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> RNA-Seq reads mapped to LH_v2.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">
                           <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> RNA-Seq expression analysis</h3><p>We carried out RNA-Seq differential expression analysis using the full complement of predicted <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genes analysed above as a guide. RNA-Seq reads were generated using 15–20 mm floral buds from four pin plants and four thrum plants (four biological replicates) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S3</a>); these plants were siblings from a controlled cross in the same population, which we reasoned would control for individual background variation. The analysis revealed 401 genes expressed in both pin and thrum flowers at different levels: 283 genes were significantly up-regulated, and 118 genes significantly down-regulated, in thrum flowers as compared to pin (FDR &lt; 0.05) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig3">3a</a>) (see <a href="http://opendata.earlham.ac.uk/primula">http://opendata.earlham.ac.uk/primula</a> for gene sequences). Four of the five thrum-specific <i>S</i> locus genes (<i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>PUM</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>KFB</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>CCM</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e1989">27</a></sup> show low expression compared to the differentially expressed geneset (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S5</a>). We also identified 525 genes expressed uniquely in thrum flowers and 468 genes expressed uniquely in pin flowers (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig3">3b</a>). For the 401 differentially expressed genes, GO-term enrichment analysis relative to GO term frequency in the full <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> geneset revealed overrepresentation of GO terms potentially related to cell wall modification and reproductive processes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig3">3c</a>). In contrast, despite a small number of genes showing relatively high expression, including the <i>S</i> locus gene <i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> (log<sub>10</sub> FPKM + 1 = 1.72) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S5</a>), there were no over-represented GO terms in the morph-specific (pin or thrum only) expression dataset (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S6</a>); this was also true using &gt;0.1 FPKM or &gt;1 FPKM minimum cutoffs for expression.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="726" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin and thrum flower differential gene expression, and GO term enrichment. (<b>a</b>) Genes with significantly higher expression (&lt;0.05 FDR) in thrum (upregulated in thrum) (n = 283) (red), or pin (downregulated in thrum) (n = 118) (black) flowers, with log<sub>2</sub> fold-change in expression in thrum compared to pin shown (FPKM, Fragments Per Kilobase of transcript per Million fragments mapped); genes expressed in only one morph are excluded; (<b>b</b>) Genes expressed in only one morph: log<sub>10</sub> change in expression (FPKM + 1) in response to the presence of the <i>S</i> locus in thrum; red = genes upregulated in thrum flowers (expressed in thrum only) (n = 525); black = genes downregulated in thrum flowers (expressed in pin only) (n = 468); (<b>c</b>) Gene Ontology (GO) terms assigned to the 401 significantly differentially expressed genes (a), and their associated GO-term enrichment scores (top 30 over-represented GO terms (False Discovery Rate (FDR) &lt; 0.1) are shown); enrichment score = −log<sub>10</sub>(uncorrected p-value), with the uncorrected p-value as calculated in GO-term enrichment analysis versus GO term occurrence in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> LH_v2 functional annotations.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genetic architecture of the <i>S</i> locus in <i>Primula</i> species</h3><p>The <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> long-homostyle genome assembly generated in this project provided four contigs that facilitated our previous definition of the <i>S</i> locus as a 278 kb thrum-specific (hemizygous) region<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2100">27</a></sup>, comprising a cluster of five genes present in thrum (<i>GLO</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>PUM</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>KFB</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>, <i>CCM</i><sup><i>T</i></sup>) but absent in pin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig4">4a</a>). Here, we examine whether the hemizygous genetic architecture is conserved in other <i>Primula</i> species. We mapped our genomic paired-end sequence reads from an individual <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum plant (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig4">4a</a>) to the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genome assembly incorporating the previously identified complete 455 kb assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2161">27</a></sup> of the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus (278 kb), flanking regions (171 kb), and flanking <i>CFB</i> loci (~6 kb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2175">27</a></sup>. Our results suggest that the <i>S</i> locus is also hemizygous in <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i>, revealed by a notable drop in coverage compared to the flanking regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig4">4a</a>). This suggests the hemizygous genetic architecture of the <i>Primula S</i> locus is an important feature that is evolutionary conserved. The <i>de novo</i> assembly of the complete <i>S</i> locus region in <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> was not possible using published <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> genome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Nowak, M. D. et al. The draft genome of Primula veris yields insight into the molecular basis of heterostyly. Genome biology 16, 16 (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2214">14</a></sup>, or our <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum read library; alignments reveal a fragmented array of assembled contigs. However, the above genomic analyses demonstrate the utility of our <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus assembly as a reference for investigating evolutionary conservation of the region in related <i>Primula</i> species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="837" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><i>Primula veris S</i> locus genetic architecture and gene structures. (<b>a</b>) Genomic read depth across the 455 kb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus assembly region in 5 kb non-overlapping windows: <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum (blue), <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> thrum (red) and pin (black); grey vertical lines above the x-axis represent ambiguous bases (“N”s) in the assembled sequence. The schematic above the graph shows the approximate size and location of <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2274">27</a></sup> within this region: thrum-specific <i>S</i> locus genes (red), tandem-repeat <i>CFB</i> loci that flank the 278 kb <i>S</i> locus (yellow), and predicted genes flanking the <i>S</i> locus (black) are indicated. (<b>b</b>) gene structures of <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> (<i>Pve</i>) orthologues of the five genes identified in the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris S</i> locus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2310">27</a></sup>. Exons (thick lines) and introns (thin lines; approximate size) are shown; introns &gt;0.5 kb are displayed as 0.5 kb (see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S4</a> for expanded intron size schematic).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Further investigation of <i>S</i> locus genomic read depth was carried out for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i>, and the more distantly related <i>P</i>. <i>farinosa</i>, <i>P</i>. <i>scotica</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#MOESM1">S1</a>), and <i>P</i>. <i>forbesii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Huu, C. N. et al. Presence versus absence of CYP734A50 underlies the style-length dimorphism in primroses. eLife 5, e17956 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2365">28</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5</a>). To avoid read mismapping for distant species, we examined coding sequence regions defined by predicted LH_v2 gene models only. This analysis revealed reduced read depth (coverage) at the <i>S</i> locus compared to the flanking regions in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> thrum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5a</a>), and zero coverage in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5b</a>), as might be expected<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2394">27</a></sup>. <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> homostyle plants originating from Somerset (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5c</a>) and Chiltern (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5d</a>) populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Crosby, J. L. High proportions of homostyle plants in populations of Primula vulgaris. Nature 145, 672–673 (1940)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2411">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Crosby, J. L. Selection of an unfavourable gene complex. Evolutionary Ecology Research 3, 212–230 (1949)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2414">32</a></sup> show diploid read coverage across the <i>S</i> locus. Both these homostyle mutants are self-fertile, and genetic analysis (not shown) reveals they are homozygous diploid for the <i>S</i> locus carrying <i>CYP</i><sup><i>T</i></sup> mutations. <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum shows reduced coverage at the <i>S</i> locus (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5e</a>), as observed in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> thrum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Li, J. et al. Genetic architecture and evolution of the S locus supergene in Primula vulgaris. Nature Plants 2, 16188 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2450">27</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-018-36304-4/MediaObjects/41598_2018_36304_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="653" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><i>Primula S</i> locus genomic read depth across coding sequence positions. Genomic read depth across predicted coding sequences (CDSs) in the 455 kb <i>S</i> locus and flanking regions in different <i>Primula</i> morphs and species: <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> thrum (<i>S</i> locus hemizygote) <b>(a)</b>, pin (<i>S</i> locus null) (<b>b</b>), long homostyle Somerset (LH-S) (<i>S</i> locus homozygote) (<b>c</b>), long homostyle Chiltern (LH-C) (<i>S</i> locus homozygote) (<b>d</b>); <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i> thrum (<b>e</b>); <i>P</i>. <i>farinosa</i> thrum (<b>f</b>); <i>P</i>. <i>scotica</i> (homostyle species) (<b>g</b>); <i>P</i>. <i>forbesii</i> thrum (<b>h</b>). Black vertical dotted lines define CDSs within the 278 kb <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> thrum-specific <i>S</i> locus. Horizontal dotted lines indicate median read depth for the <i>S</i> locus (red), and its flanking regions (blue), with these values labelled on the y-axis (small black text); these lines overlap for Chiltern long homostyle (d); <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin (b) has zero coverage across the <i>S</i> locus CDSs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For <i>P</i>. <i>farinosa</i> thrum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5f</a>), <i>P</i>. <i>scotica</i> (homostyle species) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5g</a>) and <i>P</i>. <i>forbessii</i> thrum (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5h</a>), there are fewer reads mapped to the <i>S</i> locus, but the coverage profile is nonetheless distinct from the zero coverage observed for <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> pin (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5b</a>) from which the <i>S</i> locus is absent. Reduced coverage at the <i>S</i> locus for <i>P</i>. <i>farinosa</i> (diploid) and <i>P</i>. <i>forbesii</i> (assumed diploid) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5f,g</a>) suggests this region could also be hemizygous in thrum for these species, as in <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> and <i>P</i>. <i>veris</i>. Figure <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5h</a> was compiled using <i>P</i>. <i>forbesii</i> genomic read data from BioProject PRJNA317964<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Huu, C. N. et al. Presence versus absence of CYP734A50 underlies the style-length dimorphism in primroses. eLife 5, e17956 (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2660">28</a></sup>. Reduced read coverage to the left of the <i>S</i> locus may be due to similarity with other genomic regions, a result of “N”s (ambiguous bases) in surrounding sequences, or an incorrect gene prediction. <i>P</i>. <i>scotica</i> is a naturally occurring hexaploid homostyle species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wedderburn, F. M. &amp; Richards, A. J. Secondary homostyly in Primula L.; evidence for the model of the ‘S’ supergene. New Phytologist 121, 649–655 (1992)." href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d1397e2674">43</a></sup>; rather than showing comparable coverage within and outside the <i>S</i> locus as for the <i>P</i>. <i>vulgaris</i> homostyles (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5c,d</a>) it displays reduced coverage within the central region (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-018-36304-4#Fig5">5g</a>), which might suggest <i>P</i>. <i>scotica</i> retains one or more pin chromosomes within its hexaploid karyotype. The above analyses reveal reduced coverage at the <i>S</i> locus in all <i>Primula</i> species tested.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">
